<div id="review_1428408541" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1428408541" />
    <a title="BrokenTune" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10592271-brokentune"><img alt="BrokenTune" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1391017756p2/10592271.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1428408541?book_show_action=true">Oct 29, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="BrokenTune" class="user" itemprop="url" name="BrokenTune" href="/user/show/10592271-brokentune">BrokenTune</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/561371.The_Penelopiad">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10592271-brokentune?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1428408541" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17101285362152945416">The Penelopiad is another installment of the <a href='http://www.canongate.tv/canongate-myths' rel='nofollow noopener'>Canongate Myths Series</a>.<br/><br/>In this installment, Margaret Atwood turns her hand to the story of Odysseus and tells the story of The Odyssey and The Iliad from the perspective of Penelope, Odysseus' wife.<br/><br/>If you have ever wondered what it would be like to read an Atwood version of Homer's anthem to heroism, it was fun. I read this whilst waiting at the garage. Apparently, my chuckling along persuaded the elderly gentleman next to me to co-read while waiting </span>
  <span id="freeText17101285362152945416" style="display:none">The Penelopiad is another installment of the <a href="http://www.canongate.tv/canongate-myths" rel="nofollow noopener">Canongate Myths Series</a>.<br /><br />In this installment, Margaret Atwood turns her hand to the story of Odysseus and tells the story of The Odyssey and The Iliad from the perspective of Penelope, Odysseus' wife.<br /><br />If you have ever wondered what it would be like to read an Atwood version of Homer's anthem to heroism, it was fun. I read this whilst waiting at the garage. Apparently, my chuckling along persuaded the elderly gentleman next to me to co-read while waiting for our cars to be ready. <br /><br />My favourite chapter had the title "Helen Ruins My Life", which was of course about Helen of Troy being a total tramp and running off with Paris, which in turn caused Odysseus to leave Penelope and join the war against Troy.<br /><i><br />"I've often wondered whether, if Helen hadn't been so puffed up with vanity, we might all have been spared the sufferings and sorrows she brought down on our heads by her selfishness and her deranged lust. Why couldn't she have led a normal life? But no - normal lives were boring, and Helen was ambitious. She wanted to make a name for herself. She longed to stand out from the herd."<br /></i><br />Anyway it was an entertaining read and - as usual for Atwood - emphasised a few new perspectives on an old classic.<br /><br /><i>"It's surprising how many women there are in the Odyssey and they all help Odysseus, which is why I made him so charming. He's the kind of guy women like - he has a lovely voice, he takes an interest in them, he understands human nature. That's why he's so persuasive: he doesn't get his way by force, he's not a thug. He was fun to be around. That's why Penelope is sad he's not there. He's helped by women at every turn: by Helen in The Iliad, and by all the goddesses he meets along the way in the Odyssey. And then there's Penelope holding the fort while he's away. That's the kind of guy he was."</i><br /><br />(Margaret Atwood interviewed in <a href="http://www.theguardian.com/stage/2005/oct/26/theatre.classics" rel="nofollow noopener">The Guardian</a>, 26. Oct. 2005)</span>
  <a data-text-id="17101285362152945416" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_21016359" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/21016359" />
    <a title="Teresa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/541416-teresa"><img alt="Teresa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1570841350p2/541416.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/21016359?book_show_action=true">Apr 25, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Teresa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Teresa" href="/user/show/541416-teresa">Teresa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer21016359" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15621558858818212304">Margaret Atwood has a brilliant mind, and I think this book is a brilliant way to start the Canongate Myth series: with a story that illustrates the various ways a myth can be interpreted. <br/><br/>Who is telling the truth? Is there only one truth? If so, can it be known? This may seem like a slight read, but underneath it has a lot to say about these questions; about the nature of silence; and storytelling itself, including unexpected danger when perhaps you think you've got everything covered ...</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_35549311" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/35549311" />
    <a title="Christine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1605591-christine"><img alt="Christine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1252166169p2/1605591.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/35549311?book_show_action=true">Oct 17, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Christine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Christine" href="/user/show/1605591-christine">Christine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1605591-christine?shelf=matter-of-troy">matter-of-troy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1605591-christine?shelf=minority-and-women-writers">minority-and-women-writers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1605591-christine?shelf=fantasy-myth-and-fairy">fantasy-myth-and-fairy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer35549311" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer443807345316135298">Penelope gets the shaft. Come to think of it so does Clymmenstra. Let’s get that clear before we go any further.<br/><br/>Homer was on to something when the he composed the most famous of all ancient works in the forms of the Iliad and the Odyssey. I doubt he even knew how long the story would last and how often his name would be invoked in despair by students the world over. Today, Troy and its related matter appear in a variety of forms from movies, video games, to various books.<br/><br/>This fame of the story h</span>
  <span id="freeText443807345316135298" style="display:none">Penelope gets the shaft. Come to think of it so does Clymmenstra. Let’s get that clear before we go any further.<br /><br />Homer was on to something when the he composed the most famous of all ancient works in the forms of the Iliad and the Odyssey. I doubt he even knew how long the story would last and how often his name would be invoked in despair by students the world over. Today, Troy and its related matter appear in a variety of forms from movies, video games, to various books.<br /><br />This fame of the story has allowed various writers, starting way back when, to add on to or revise parts of the story. No, Helen wasn’t in Troy; she was in Egypt, and so on. Apparently Helen and Achilles got together after they died. Go figure.<br /><br />Who seems to be left out of most revisions, or pushed to the sidelines are Penelope and Clymmenstra. This is even true in the modern where one would think the advent of feminist would allow for redemption of Clymmenstra, who after all, sought to avenge herself on the husband who had murdered her eldest daughter. Murdered the girl after telling his wife and daughter, that she would be married to the great Achilles. It’s a worse switcheroo than what More did to his second wife. Nope, Clymmenstra gets the shaft.<br /><br />Penelope, too, gets the shaft. True, while she has her trials and tribulations, they aren’t as shocking or tragic as Clymmenstra. But she was, for all purposes, a single mum, left behind for twenty years while her husband fought, sexed, and sailed his way across the Greek world. Penelope was patient Griselda, while Odysseus was a playa. Does anyone really think she was down with that?<br /><br />Helen, on the contrary, gets all the attention. The woman, whose irresponsible behavior started a war and ruined not only a city but thousands of lives, gets excused. It wasn’t her. It was true love. It was all Aphrodite’s fault. Nope, it was Menelaus because he was an old, ugly, bastard. Of course, it wasn’t like Paris was completely free either. But none of it is Helen’s fault who unlike her sister and cousin abandoned her child.<br /><br />Don’t we usually get upset when women do that? Beauty is a perfect excuse isn’t it? Helen, for me, will always be that actress in the version of the <i>The Trojan Women</i> starring Katherine Hepburn. Doesn’t say a word, just disrobes, gets husband number one back, while the other women are enslaved, killed, raped, and can’t bury their dead.<br /><br />Clymmenstra’s fault, besides her lack of beauty, is her rage. Penelope’s, her cunning. Such women are not to be celebrated. This is true even today. Look at the different between quirky man doctors and detectives, and their female counterparts.<br /><br />See, what I mean.<br /><br />It’s lucky for us that we have Margaret Atwood for she takes the character of Penelope and her maids for the center of her novel, <i>The Penelopiad</i>.<br /><br />Atwood draws on more material than just Homer. She makes use of the notes in Robert Graves’ work, and makes use of the Greek Chorus. What we get is a story where the home life is front and center, and the wanderer, the usual; star is kept offstage for as long as possible, which is rather easy.<br /><br />The Penelope that Atwood presents may or may not be a faithful wife. We don’t know. Does she? Who knows? The Penelope that Atwood presents does her best in a male dominated and controlled world. Separated from her home and then left to raise her son, she must later struggle with a teenage who could use a good hiding as well as suitors who supposedly love her beauty but eat her out of house and home. <br /><br />Her maids have it worse. After all, how important are they? They are women and aren’t even noble. Then they get punished by their master. By a man who took part in the sack of city and who surely most know the fate of women at the hands of the more powerful. Penelope is more like one of her cousins than she thought. She should have taken a lover and murdered her husband in the bath. Come back after years of affairs with goddesses and nymphs? Take that you bastard! I rule now!<br /><br />But Penelope is water, so that isn’t her way. Her cunning also never gets as much notice as that as her husband. Perhaps because her battle is a domestic one. But that bit with the shroud, what man would have thought of it?<br /><br />By giving Penelope and the maids a voice, Atwood inserts a needed piece into the picture of Troy and the Odyssey. She adds another narrative thread, and hopefully, her work will be remembered as long as Homer’s.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="443807345316135298" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_716930554" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/716930554" />
    <a title="Ova - Excuse My Reading" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5316099-ova---excuse-my-reading"><img alt="Ova - Excuse My Reading" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1543876995p2/5316099.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/716930554?book_show_action=true">Sep 11, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ova - Excuse My Reading" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ova - Excuse My Reading" href="/user/show/5316099-ova---excuse-my-reading">Ova - Excuse My Reading</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer716930554" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15539340898400033809">It's always a joy to read a poetic story telling by Atwood, and Penelopiad works like a charm. An elegant short novel of Greek myth.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_review_716930554'><a id="like_count_review_716930554" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/716930554?resource_type=Review"><span class="likesCount">26 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it gr-button gr-button--small" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F17645.The_Penelopiad">Like</a></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_255713993" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/255713993" />
    <a title="David Lentz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3604115-david-lentz"><img alt="David Lentz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1473090881p2/3604115.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/255713993?book_show_action=true">Jan 03, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Lentz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Lentz" href="/user/show/3604115-david-lentz">David Lentz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer255713993" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8829274773205048996">I was intrigued to read a woman's point of view focused upon one of the great heroines of Homer's "Odyssey" in Penelope. Homer's work is, of course, an epic masterpiece which has endured for more than four milennia and it is nearly impossible to do justice to this legend of incredible, ancient genius. I was disappointed in several places by Atwood's rather shallow depictions of the characters of both Odysseus and Penelope who were both courageous and brilliant in their own ways in Homer's tale. </span>
  <span id="freeText8829274773205048996" style="display:none">I was intrigued to read a woman's point of view focused upon one of the great heroines of Homer's "Odyssey" in Penelope. Homer's work is, of course, an epic masterpiece which has endured for more than four milennia and it is nearly impossible to do justice to this legend of incredible, ancient genius. I was disappointed in several places by Atwood's rather shallow depictions of the characters of both Odysseus and Penelope who were both courageous and brilliant in their own ways in Homer's tale. Penelope in "Penelopiad" deems herself as bright enough but has issues with her self-image as Helen's cousin and who wouldn't? Compared to Helen, whose outer beauty heaped a decade of carnage upon two great ancient civilizations, Penelope considers herself as virtually a hag. Penelope is in her mid- to late-thirties, a woman's prime, and chased ardently by 120 young suitors who sought her for political advantages and because in my view she was a true beauty. Homer's Penelope was incredibly loyal to her husband, extremely intelligent, shrewd in her knitting and unknitting the shroud, a loving mother and managed to keep her enemies at bay until the return of Odysseus after 20 years away from home at and returning from the Trojan War. Homer treats Penelope respectfully and heroically but Atwood in the first person singular diminishes her perhaps to project the virtue of modesty but, as she narrates as a Shade in Hades, Atwood's Penelope pulls no punches. I wish that she could have retained the heroic idea of Penelope from Homer, which Atwood diminishes for me in a way that Joyce in "Ulysses," despite Molly Bloom's infidelity, manages to mantain. Atwood exagerates the economic advantages in the suitors' pursuits of Penelope and, thereby, diminishes their pure physical ardor for her. The Odysseus of Homer is utterly mad for his beautiful, adoring, loyal and saintly wife. Atwood seems coy, self-absorded and even cute, at times, in her rendering of this heroic, epic tale. As for Odysseus he is, of course, massively flawed and flayed in Atwood's "Penelopiad" and criticized for his short legs and barrel chest a few too many times. In Homer remember that Odysseus is the genius strategist behind the Trojan Horse who helps turn the trajectory of the Trojan War. Odysseus ultimately honors his oath to place himself at mortal risk to stand with Menelaus who was wronged by Paris and the Trojans. Odysseus is courageous, resourceful, strategically gifted, charismatic, articulate, skilled as a warrior, powerful in battle, decisive, adept in crisis, inventive, fit and a king. Atwood blows him off as a second-rate hero to diminish Odysseus, Penelope and herself as an author. Of course, there is the killing of the 120 suitors and the hanging of the maids. The suitors were working as hard as possible to steal the wife and property of Odysseus and kill his son while Odysseus was away fighting a war. I have little sympathy for the suitors in this ancient tale. But the hanging of the maids in typically Greek tragic fashion cannot be forgiven or even properly explained by either Atwood or her Penelope. Nevermind that Odysseus was lied to by other women of his family and unaware of their complicity with Penelope in her ruses to fend off the suitors. Tragically, Odysseus must live forever with his tragic mistake as he walks the regions of Hades seeking to avoid these maidens with whose plight I very much sympathized. Their killing reveals a tragic flaw in Odysseus which, again, Atwood never fairly addresses. Although Atwood has been rather scholarly in most of her depiction of the "Odyssey," she seems to have failed to study "The Iliad" nearly half as well. The reason that Odysseus is so tragically flawed with regard to the 12 maidens is "The Iliad" itself -- the brutal, barbaric, inhuman and extended decade of war against the Trojans. Homer's catalogue of the epic brutality of this war is clearly evident throughout "The Iliad" and Odysseus cannot escape its tragic effects upon himself. Call it post-traumatic stress disorder and consider its impact upon our own heroic American soldiers in Iraq and Afghanistan after a decade of brutal war. Odysseus survives tens years of war with Troy and ten years of peril on the sea but cannot escape the effect of two decades of war upon his own psyche and Penelope/ Atwood have no clue about this reality. The sheer and utter tragedy of the hanging of the maids plays itself out by American heroes returning from war with post-traumatic stress disorder. In both epic tales the hero metamorphoses into a fiend and, although he survives the war, even in victory Odysseus cannot ultimately evade its inhumanely, tragic after-effects. The perceptive layers of tragedy reinforce blind Homer in "The Odyssey" as a true genius and in my estimation render Atwood in "Penelopiad" guilty of literary hubris. Atwood's novel does not begin to do justice to the epic poem and seems cavalier, coy and cute in attempting to do so without paying real homage to the heroic figures of Homer with a true depth of vision and understanding that Joyce so competently manages to advance with his genius in "Ulysses." I very much respect Atwood for her work in "The Handmaid's Tale" but my best advice to readers is to pass on this one in favor of reading Homer's "Odyssey" and Joyce's "Ulysses" as well as Atwood's far more worthy "Handmaid's Tale."</span>
  <a data-text-id="8829274773205048996" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText8875806228311569736" style="display:none">An interesting exploration of the back story behind Homer's tale.<br /><br />Penelope is left behind when Odysseus goes off to the Trojan war. For ten years of war and then a further ten years of gallivanting around the Med, Penelope waits patiently at home for her man.<br /><br />Odysseus gets to fight Cyclops, dodge sirens and sleep with goddesses, whilst Penelope brings up their son - and a ravenous set of youths circle around the rich "widow". She gets her maids to see to the amorous suitors whilst desperately seeking news of Odysseus.<br /><br />Odysseus gets home, the youths are slaughtered, and a dozen maids are strung up for good measure - for sleeping with the enemy. Penelope is held up as a beacon - a dutiful, chaste wife, as all wives should be. <br /><br />Or is she?<br /><br />Double standards - yes. Ambiguous - yes.<br /><br />An interesting read, hampered somewhat by my lack of knowledge of the myth. It has certainly whetted my appetite to find out a little more about the original story.<br /></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer6842058171341367942">Very clever execution.<br/><br/>Atwood's reimagining of Penelope from the Odyssey, who never speaks a single word is fascinating and an interesting character study. Odysseus's wife, and yes! Atwood totally throws that weasel under the bus by calling him out to be the liar and thief he was. That said, Penelope is not an inspiring character.   <span id="freeText6842058171341367942" style="display:none">Very clever execution.<br /><br />Atwood's reimagining of Penelope from the Odyssey, who never speaks a single word is fascinating and an interesting character study. Odysseus's wife, and yes! Atwood totally throws that weasel under the bus by calling him out to be the liar and thief he was. That said, Penelope is not an inspiring character. Young when wed and very much the bend not break kind of personality means everyone else gets top billing. <br /><br />But hands down, the best and my favorite part was the Hanged Maids' chorus. In homage to ancient Greek poetry and just funny, scathing, and all around the transcendent component of this work. In the notes, Atwood acknowledges Graves' theory of lunar handmaidens, 12 and Atwood preposes Penelope as the 13th for a complete retinue of the annual cycle. Again, very interesting and now I need to track down Graves' translation with these notes. <br /><br />Hanged Maids for the WIN!<br />Penelope--meh. <br />Seriously, without the chorus I'd have rated this two stars.</span>

<div id="review_1800694" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1800694" />
    <a title="katie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/81540-katie"><img alt="katie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1181415317p2/81540.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1800694?book_show_action=true">Jun 09, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="katie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="katie" href="/user/show/81540-katie">katie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/561371.The_Penelopiad">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/81540-katie?shelf=read_07">read_07</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/81540-katie?shelf=re-imagining-is-the-same-as-fanfic">re-imagining-is-the-same-as-fanfic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/81540-katie?shelf=greek-myth">greek-myth</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1800694" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14006927574843882346">I tried. I know no one's going to believe me, but I tried to like this, I swear. I didn't, though. Frankly, the fact that I made it through the book is an accomplishment.<br/><br/>I guess it's a classic Margaret Atwood theme that women are mean to each other. And I guess, it's an indictment? That if it's bad when men treat women poorly (and it is), that it's utterly inforgivable when women treat other women poorly. But, I don't know that shoving that theme onto these characters serves anyone very well. No</span>
  <span id="freeText14006927574843882346" style="display:none">I tried. I know no one's going to believe me, but I tried to like this, I swear. I didn't, though. Frankly, the fact that I made it through the book is an accomplishment.<br /><br />I guess it's a classic Margaret Atwood theme that women are mean to each other. And I guess, it's an indictment? That if it's bad when men treat women poorly (and it is), that it's utterly inforgivable when women treat other women poorly. But, I don't know that shoving that theme onto these characters serves anyone very well. Nothing was more grating in the novel than Penelope's crazed obsessive jelousy of Helen and Helen's stereotypical gorgeous-woman putdowns, sniping, and narcissism. It made neither of them sympathetic enough to spend a whole book with.<br /><br />I actually think that exploring Penelope's role in the murder of her maids is a compelling and unexpected choice to make when interrogating the text from a feminist perspective-- it's certainly not the "Penelope as stereotypical faithful wife was just a tool to keep the patriarchy afloat" angle that I expected. In the early parts of the novel, I actually enjoyed the Chorus of the 12 hanged maids quite a lot. Both as a literary device to comment on Penelope's version of the story and the actual poems/songs themselves. However, I wish Atwood had chosen to make a clearer statement about the culpability of Penelope, rather than essentially boiling it down to "slave girls sure have it bad."<br /><br />Despite all my not-at-all surprising issues with the themes and ideas of the text, the true dealbreaker, the thing that I could not move past, was the writing style. It's first-person, the tale told by Penelope in modern days as she wanders through Hades. I'm not wild about first-person to begin with, and I think having her clearly address a modern audience makes the whole thing way more didactic than it needs to be. More than anything, though, the little witticisms and commentary Atwood attempts to make about our society are completely transparent, never clever or funny, and pull the reader out of the story in a way that I don't think is intentional. The one about steroids leaps to mind as particularly glaring. <br /><br />I had really low expectations going in and yet it still managed to disappoint. [sigh]</span>
  <a data-text-id="14006927574843882346" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2792811994" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1541047868864104207">I was initially nervous before reading The Penelopiad as I’ve mentioned before my Greek mythology knowledge is scant, but I should have known better. Atwood’s handling of Penelope’s voice was fantastic and brought it right into the 21st century while enlightening me to the story of Odysseus. She challenges the way Greek myths are recounted, by giving a voice to Penelope and her 12 handmaids with her usual wit and sass. I almost had to put the book down and slow clap her in my living room when sh</span>
  <span id="freeText1541047868864104207" style="display:none">I was initially nervous before reading The Penelopiad as I’ve mentioned before my Greek mythology knowledge is scant, but I should have known better. Atwood’s handling of Penelope’s voice was fantastic and brought it right into the 21st century while enlightening me to the story of Odysseus. She challenges the way Greek myths are recounted, by giving a voice to Penelope and her 12 handmaids with her usual wit and sass. I almost had to put the book down and slow clap her in my living room when she called out the way rape trials are more concerned with protecting the ‘reputations’ of the perpetrator rather than seeking justice for the victims. Because that’s how Atwood does an Iliad retelling. And I loved it. I wish it was longer!</span>
  <a data-text-id="1541047868864104207" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('s3k9CoVHOVrnHYQvPg/qKKGBbs1c+EIXWNJGVaJLBjpJmkGtryydNCmBBstC0uEDGNouMa7Ob6MWgiWe8onO3g==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('C/I8MoSeQqmY5oTjvQnBB2BKczpVTJgQNf0vXpbEQS3xEUCVrvXmx1Z6BgfB1Mos2REzxqd6taR7rUyVxgaJyQ==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

It was the story of all of them…\n\nIn the middle of the night, Creusa wakes to find her beloved Troy engulfed in flames. Ten seemingly endless years of brutal conflict between the Greeks and the Trojans are over, and the Greeks are victorious. Over the next few hours, the only life she has ever known will turn to ash . . .\n\nThe devastating consequences of the fall of Troy stretch from Mount Olympus to Mount Ida, from the citadel of Troy to the distant Greek islands, and across oceans and sky in between. These are the stories of the women embroiled in that legendary war and its terrible aftermath, as well as the feud and the fatal decisions that started it all…\n\nPowerfully told from an all-female And still, today, a wealth of novels, plays and films draw their inspiration from stories first told almost three thousand years ago. But modern tellers of Greek myth have usually been men, and have routinely shown little interest in telling women’s stories.\n\nNow, in Pandora’s Jar, Natalie Haynes – broadcaster, writer and passionate classicist – redresses this imbalance. Taking Greek creation myths as her starting point and then retelling the four great mythic sagas: the Trojan War, the Royal House of Thebes, Jason and the Argonauts, Heracles, she puts the female characters on equal footing with their menfolk. The result is a vivid and powerful account of the deeds – and misdeeds - of Hera, Aphrodite, Athene and Circe. And away from the goddesses of Mount Olympus it is Helen, Clytemnestra, Jocasta, Antigone and Medea who sing from these pages, not Paris, Agamemnon, Orestes or Jason.<\/span>\n  <a       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/3472.Margaret_Atwood">About Margaret Atwood</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer14564042761621485003">Margaret Atwood was born in 1939 in Ottawa and grew up in northern Ontario, Quebec, and Toronto. She received her undergraduate degree from Victoria College at the University of Toronto and her master's degree from Radcliffe College.<br/><br/>Throughout her writing career, Margaret Atwood has received numerous awards and honourary degrees. She is the author of more than thirty-five volumes of poetry, childr</span>
  <span id="freeText14564042761621485003" style="display:none">Margaret Atwood was born in 1939 in Ottawa and grew up in northern Ontario, Quebec, and Toronto. She received her undergraduate degree from Victoria College at the University of Toronto and her master's degree from Radcliffe College.<br /><br />Throughout her writing career, Margaret Atwood has received numerous awards and honourary degrees. She is the author of more than thirty-five volumes of poetry, children’s literature, fiction, and non-fiction and is perhaps best known for her novels, which include The Edible Woman (1970), The Handmaid's Tale (1983), The Robber Bride (1994), Alias Grace (1996), and The Blind Assassin, which won the prestigious Booker Prize in 2000. Atwood's dystopic novel, Oryx and Crake, was published in 2003. The Tent (mini-fictions) and Moral Disorder (short stories) both appeared in 2006. Her most recent volume of poetry, The Door, was published in 2007. Her non-fiction book, Payback: Debt and the Shadow Side of Wealth ­ in the Massey series, appeared in 2008, and her most recent novel, The Year of the Flood, in the autumn of 2009. Ms. Atwood's work has been published in more than forty languages, including Farsi, Japanese, Turkish, Finnish, Korean, Icelandic and Estonian. In 2004 she co-invented the Long Pen TM.<br /><br />Margaret Atwood currently lives in Toronto with writer Graeme Gibson. <br /><br />Associations: Margaret Atwood was President of the Writers' Union of Canada from May 1981 to May 1982, and was President of International P.E.N., Canadian Centre (English Speaking) from 1984-1986. She and Graeme Gibson are the Joint Honourary Presidents of the Rare Bird Society within BirdLife International. Ms. Atwood is also a current Vice-President of PEN International.<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14564042761621485003" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/3016476-the-penelopiad">Quotes from The Penelopiad</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Water does not resist. Water flows. When you plunge your hand into it, all you feel is a caress. Water is not a solid wall, it will not stop you. But water always goes where it wants to go, and nothing in the end can stand against it. Water is patient. Dripping water wears away a stone. Remember that, my child. Remember you are half water. If you can't go through an obstacle, go around it. Water does.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/417813-water-does-not-resist-water-flows-when-you-plunge-your">1102 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Cleverness is a quality a man likes to have in his wife as long as she is some distance away from him. Up close, he'll take kindness any day of the week, if there's nothing more alluring to be had.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/4369-cleverness-is-a-quality-a-man-likes-to-have-in">122 likes</a>
